import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    A model that performs a 3D transposed convolution, followed by batch normalization, 
    two average pooling layers.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding)
        self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm3d(out_channels)
        self.avg_pool1 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2)
        self.avg_pool2 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = self.batch_norm(x)
        x = self.avg_pool1(x)
        x = self.avg_pool2(x)
        return x


batch_size = 64
in_channels = 3
out_channels = 16
depth, height, width = 32, 32, 32
kernel_size = 3
stride = 2
padding = 1
bias_shape = (out_channels, 1, 1, 1)

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape]

So u[-1] = 0. So y[0] = k[1]*x[0] (since u[0]=x[0], u[-1]=0, u[1]=0). And y[1] = sum k[p]*u[2-p] = k[0]*u[2] + k[1]*u[1] + k[2]*u[0] = k[0]*x[1] + k[2]*x[0]. So z[0] = (y[0] + y[1])/2 = (k[1]*x[0] + k[0]*x[1] + k[2]*x[0])/2 = ((k[1]+k[2])*x[0] + k[0]*x[1])/2. So z[i] for general i is a weighted combination of x[i] and x[i+1] (and maybe x[i-1]). Let me be more systematic. y[2i] = k[1]*x[i] (using u[2i]=x[i], u[2i+1]=0, u[2i-1]=0) Actually wait: y[2i] = k[0]*u[2i+1] + k[1]*u[2i] + k[2]*u[2i-1] = k[0]*0 + k[1]*x[i] + k[2]*0 = k[1]*x[i] But what about boundaries? For i=0: u[-1]=0. For i=31: y[62]=k[1]*x[31]. Yes. So z[i] = (k[1]*x[i] + k[0]*x[i+1] + k[2]*x[i])/2 = ((k[1]+k[2])*x[i] + k[0]*x[i+1])/2. This is interesting! After one transposed conv and one avg pool, the result is equivalent to a simple conv-like operation on the original input. But wait, this is for 1D. For 3D, it would be a product of such operations across dimensions. Actually, in 3D with separable kernels across dimensions (if the kernel is separable), but in general it's not. The general 3D kernel is [kd, kh, kw]. However, the above derivation shows that y[d,h,w] for even d only depends on input at depth=d/2 with kh=1 (in the conv sense), and y for odd d depends on input at depths (d+1)/2 and (d-1)/2 with kh=0 and kh=2. After averaging over pairs of d, the result z[d/2, ...] is a linear combination of adjacent input depths. Similarly for h and w. So after two avg pools (averaging over 4 values in each dimension), the final output is a weighted combination of a 3x3x3 neighborhood in the original input, with weights derived from the original transposed conv kernel. This means we could potentially replace the entire sequence with a single standard convolution on the input! That's a huge optimization. Let me verify this algebraically. In 1D after two pools: First pool z1[i] = (y[2i] + y[2i+1])/2 for i=0..30. Second pool on z1 of size 31: z2[j] = (z1[2j] + z1[2j+1])/2 for j=0..14. For j: z2[j] = (z1[2j] + z1[2j+1])/2 = ((y[4j] + y[4j+1])/2 + (y[4j+2] + y[4j+3])/2)/2 = (y[4j] + y[4j+1] + y[4j+2] + y[4j+3])/4 And we computed: y[ even ] = k[1]*x[even/2] y[ odd ] = k[0]*x[(odd+1)/2] + k[2]*x[(odd-1)/2] So: y[4j] = k[1]*x[2j] y[4j+1] = k[0]*x[2j+1] + k[2]*x[2j] y[4j+2] = k[1]*x[2j+1] y[4j+3] = k[0]*x[2j+2] + k[2]*x[2j+1] Sum = k[1]*x[2j] + k[0]*x[2j+1] + k[2]*x[2j] + k[1]*x[2j+1] + k[0]*x[2j+2] + k[2]*x[2j+1] = (k[1]+k[2])*x[2j] + (k[0]+k[1]+k[2])*x[2j+1] + k[0]*x[2j+2] Wait, that doesn't look like a simple convolution. Let me double check. Actually: y[4j] = k[1]*x[2j] y[4j+1] = k[0]*x[2j+1] + k[2]*x[2j] y[4j+2] = k[1]*x[2j+1] y[4j+3] = k[0]*x[2j+2] + k[2]*x[2j+1] Sum = k[1]*x[2j] + k[0]*x[2j+1] + k[2]*x[2j] + k[1]*x[2j+1] + k[0]*x[2j+2] + k[2]*x[2j+1] = k[1]*x[2j] + k[2]*x[2j] + k[0]*x[2j+1] + k[1]*x[2j+1] + k[2]*x[2j+1] + k[0]*x[2j+2] = (k[1]+k[2])*x[2j] + (k[0]+k[1]+k[2])*x[2j+1] + k[0]*x[2j+2] So z2[j] = [(k[1]+k[2])*x[2j] + (k[0]+k[1]+k[2])*x[2j+1] + k[0]*x[2j+2]] / 4 This is a 1D convolution with kernel [(k[1]+k[2])/4, (k[0]+k[1]+k[2])/4, k[0]/4] applied to x, but with output stride 2! Wait, the output index is j, and it depends on x[2j], x[2j+1], x[2j+2]. Hmm, that's not a standard strided convolution. It's like a convolution followed by downsampling by 2, but the receptive field spans 3 input elements. Actually, that's exactly what a strided convolution with kernel size 3 and stride 2 does! Wait, a strided conv with kernel [a, b, c] and stride 2 computes: output[j] = a*x[2j] + b*x[2j+1] + c*x[2j+2] (with appropriate padding). So yes! After transposed conv with stride 2, kernel k[0..2], followed by two avg pools of size 2 stride 2, the 1D operation is equivalent to a strided conv with stride 2 and kernel: [k[1]+k[2], k[0]+k[1]+k[2], k[0]] / 4 with appropriate boundary handling. For 3D, the full operation would be a separable combination across dimensions. The effective kernel would be the outer product of the 1D effective kernels. So for the 3D case, the effective kernel in each dimension is: kd_eff[0] = (k[1] + k[2]) / 4 for the first position (corresponding to input at offset 2j) Wait, I need to be more careful with the indexing. Actually, for the 3D case, each output position (j_d, j_h, j_w) depends on input positions: d: [2*j_d, 2*j_d+1, 2*j_d+2] h: [2*j_h, 2*j_h+1, 2*j_h+2] w: [2*j_w, 2*j_w+1, 2*j_w+2] So a 3x3x3 block of input voxels. The weights are: For dimension d with kernel k_d[0..2], the effective 1D weights are: w_d[0] = (k_d[1] + k_d[2]) / 4 w_d[1] = (k_d[0] + k_d[1] + k_d[2]) / 4 w_d[2] = k_d[0] / 4 where w_d[0] multiplies x[2*j_d], w_d[1] multiplies x[2*j_d+1], w_d[2] multiplies x[2*j_d+2]. Wait, but in a standard strided conv, output[j] = sum_{p=0..2} kernel[p] * input[2*j + p]. That matches what we have! So the entire sequence: ConvTranspose3d(in=3, out=16, kernel=3, stride=2, padding=1) + AvgPool3d(2) + AvgPool3d(2) is mathematically equivalent to: Conv3d(in=3, out=16, kernel=3, stride=2, padding=0) with a specific weight transformation! Let me verify this equivalence. The weight transformation for each dimension independently gives a 3-element effective weight vector, and the 3D kernel is the outer product across dimensions, multiplied by the input channel weights. More precisely, if the transposed conv kernel is K[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw], then the equivalent conv3d kernel K_eff[c_out, c_in, d, h, w] is: For 1D with kernel k[0..2]: w_eff[0] = (k[1] + k[2]) / 4 w_eff[1] = (k[0] + k[1] + k[2]) / 4 w_eff[2] = k[0] / 4 For 3D: K_eff[c_out, c_in, i, j, k] = w_d[i] * w_h[j] * w_w[k] * K[c_out, c_in, :, :, :] ? No wait, that's not right. Actually, each dimension applies independently. For the 3D case: y[d,h,w] = sum_{p,q,r} K[c_out, c_in, p, q, r] * u[d-p+1, h-q+1, w-r+1] where u is the zero-upsampled input. After the two avg pools, the output at [j_d, j_h, j_w] is a weighted sum over: d in {4j_d, 4j_d+1, 4j_d+2, 4j_d+3} h in {4j_h, 4j_h+1, 4j_h+2, 4j_h+3} w in {4j_w, 4j_w+1, 4j_w+2, 4j_w+3} Each y[d,h,w] = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * u[d-p+1, h-q+1, w-r+1] For a fixed (d,h,w), only specific (p,q,r) give non-zero u terms. In fact, because the sum over the 4x4x4 block of y values decomposes across dimensions, the total contribution can be written as: output[j_d, j_h, j_w] = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * S_d(j_d, p) * S_h(j_h, q) * S_w(j_w, r) where S_d(j, p) = sum_{d in block_j} u[d-p+1] / 64, and similarly for h, w. Wait, there's a subtlety. The avg pool divides by 8 for each pool, so total divide by 64. And u[d-p+1] is non-zero only when d-p+1 is even and in range. Let me compute S_d(j, p) for each p. Actually, 1/64 = (1/2)^3 * (1/2)^3 = 1/8 * 1/8, but for the full 3D we divide by 64 once. For the 1D sum factor, it's convenient to think of dividing by 4 for each dimension's contribution, but the actual division is by 64 overall. For the equivalent weight computation, the factor 1/64 distributes multiplicatively across dimensions: each dimension contributes a factor that's part of the total 1/64. Actually, the key insight is that sum over d,h,w decomposes into a product of 1D sums because the kernel K[p,q,r] factors out and u is defined per-dimension. output[j_d, j_h, j_w] = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * [sum_d u[d-p+1]/4] * [sum_h u[h-q+1]/4] * [sum_w u[w-r+1]/4] / 4 Wait, 4*4*4 = 64. So if each dimension sum is divided by 4, the total is divided by 64. But let me just check: sum_{d,h,w in blocks} y[d,h,w] / 64 = sum_{d,h,w} sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * u[d-p+1]*u[h-q+1]*u[w-r+1] / 64 = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * [sum_d u[d-p+1]/4] * [sum_h u[h-q+1]/4] * [sum_w u[w-r+1]/4] * (4*4*4/64) Since 4*4*4/64 = 64/64 = 1, we have: = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * A_d(j_d, p) * A_h(j_h, q) * A_w(j_w, r) where A_d(j, p) = sum_{d=4j}^{4j+3} u[d-p+1] / 4. So let me compute A_d(j, p) for p=0,1,2. A_d(j, p) = sum_{d=4j}^{4j+3} u[d-p+1] / 4 For p=0: sum_{d=4j}^{4j+3} u[d+1] / 4 d=4j: u[4j+1] = 0 (odd) d=4j+1: u[4j+2] = x[2j+1] d=4j+2: u[4j+3] = 0 d=4j+3: u[4j+4] = x[2j+2] Sum = x[2j+1] + x[2j+2] A_d(j, 0) = (x[2j+1] + x[2j+2]) / 4 For p=1: sum_{d=4j}^{4j+3} u[d] / 4 d=4j: u[4j] = x[2j] d=4j+1: u[4j+1] = 0 d=4j+2: u[4j+2] = x[2j+1] d=4j+3: u[4j+3] = 0 Sum = x[2j] + x[2j+1] A_d(j, 1) = (x[2j] + x[2j+1]) / 4 For p=2: sum_{d=4j}^{4j+3} u[d-1] / 4 d=4j: u[4j-1] = 0 d=4j+1: u[4j] = x[2j] d=4j+2: u[4j+1] = 0 d=4j+3: u[4j+2] = x[2j+1] Sum = x[2j] + x[2j+1] Wait, that's the same as p=1. For p=2 and j=0: d=1 gives u[0]=x[0]. For j=14: d=57 gives u[56]=x[28], d=59 gives u[58]=x[29]. So yes, x[2j] and x[2j+1]. And A_d(j, 0) = (x[2j+1] + x[2j+2]) / 4. So the effective weights on x for a given j are not a simple strided conv! Because A_d depends on K[p,q,r] in a more complex way. Actually, let me write out the full expansion for a specific dimension: output[j] = K[0] * (x[2j+1] + x[2j+2])/4 + K[1] * (x[2j] + x[2j+1])/4 + K[2] * (x[2j] + x[2j+1])/4 = K[0] * (x[2j+1] + x[2j+2])/4 + (K[1] + K[2]) * (x[2j] + x[2j+1])/4 = (K[1]+K[2])/4 * x[2j] + (K[0]+K[1]+K[2])/4 * x[2j+1] + K[0]/4 * x[2j+2] This is exactly the same as what I derived earlier! Great. So for the full 3D case with channel mixing: output[c_out, j_d, j_h, j_w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{p,q,r} K[c_out, c_in, p, q, r] * A_d(j_d, p, c_in) * A_h(j_h, q, c_in) * A_w(j_w, r, c_in) Wait, but A_d, A_h, A_w all depend on the same input x[c_in, ...]. So: output[c_out, j_d, j_h, j_w] = sum_{c_in} [sum_{p,q,r} K[c_out, c_in, p, q, r] * A_d(j_d, p, c_in) * A_h(j_h, q, c_in) * A_w(j_w, r, c_in)] where A_d(j, p, c_in) uses input x[c_in, 2j + offset(p)]. This can be rewritten as: = sum_{c_in} sum_{i,j,k in {0,1,2}} W[c_out, c_in, i, j, k] * x[c_in, 2*j_d+i, 2*j_h+j, 2*j_w+k] where W is the effective weight tensor. From the 1D derivation, the mapping is: For depth dimension, output depends on x at offsets 0, 1, 2 from base 2*j_d. The weights for these offsets come from summing over p,q,r. Specifically: W[..., i, j, k] = contribution coefficient for x[2*jd+i, 2*jh+j, 2*jw+k] For a 3D kernel K[p,q,r], the contribution to x[2*jd+i, ...] comes from all combinations where the depth offset is i, height offset is j, width offset is k. From depth: x[2*jd + depth_offset], where depth_offset depends on p. For p=0: depth_offset is 1 or 2 (from A_d(j,0) using x[2j+1] and x[2j+2]) For p=1: depth_offset is 0 or 1 For p=2: depth_offset is 0 or 1 Hmm, this is getting complicated. The structure is: A_d(j, p) = a_p[0] * x[2j] + a_p[1] * x[2j+1] + a_p[2] * x[2j+2] where: a_0 = [0, 1/4, 1/4] a_1 = [1/4, 1/4, 0] a_2 = [1/4, 1/4, 0] So the product A_d * A_h * A_w gives a sum over x[2j_d + i_d, 2j_h + i_h, 2j_w + i_w] with coefficients a_p[i_d] * a_q[i_h] * a_r[i_w]. And output = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * sum_{i,j,k} a_p[i] * a_q[j] * a_r[k] * x[...] = sum_{i,j,k} [sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * a_p[i] * a_q[j] * a_r[k]] * x[2j_d+i, 2j_h+j, 2j_w+k] So the effective kernel is: W[i,j,k] = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * a_p[i] * a_q[j] * a_r[k] where: a_p[i] is the coefficient matrix: i=0 i=1 i=2 p=0: 0 1/4 1/4 p=1: 1/4 1/4 0 p=2: 1/4 1/4 0 This is a linear transformation! The effective kernel W = T(K) is obtained by convolving K with the a-tensor in a specific way. For each output channel and input channel independently: W[c_out, c_in, i, j, k] = sum_{p,q,r} K[c_out, c_in, p, q, r] * a_p[i] * a_q[j] * a_r[k] This is equal to: W = K ⊗ A, where A[p, i] = a_p[i] and ⊗ is a mode-wise tensor product. We can compute W as: For each c_out, c_in: W[c_out, c_in] = tensor product of T_d * T_h * T_w applied to K[c_out, c_in] where for each dimension, it's a matrix multiplication: For fixed q,r: new_kernel[:, q, r] = A^T * K[:, q, r] etc. Actually, a_p[i] as a matrix (p rows, i columns): A = [[0, 1/4, 1/4], [1/4, 1/4, 0 ], [1/4, 1/4, 0 ]] And W[i,j,k] = sum_p A[p,i] * V[p,j,k], where V[p,j,k] = sum_{q,r} K[p,q,r] * a_q[j] * a_r[k]. So we apply A^T along each dimension of K sequentially. Since A^T is: [[0, 1/4, 1/4], [1/4, 1/4, 1/4], [1/4, 0, 0 ]] Wait, A^T[i,p] = A[p,i], so: A^T = [[0, 1/4, 1/4], [1/4, 1/4, 1/4], [1/4, 0, 0 ]] Hmm wait, A[p=0] = [0, 1/4, 1/4], A[p=1] = [1/4, 1/4, 0], A[p=2] = [1/4, 1/4, 0]. A^T columns are these rows: col0 = [0, 1/4, 1/4] col1 = [1/4, 1/4, 1/4] col2 = [1/4, 0, 0] So A^T = [[0, 1/4, 1/4], [1/4, 1/4, 0 ], [1/4, 0, 0 ]] Wait, row 2 of A^T is A[:,2] = [1/4, 0, 0]. Yes. So W = A^T ⊗ A^T ⊗ A^T (K), meaning apply A^T mode-1, mode-2, mode-3. This means: W[i,j,k] = sum_{p,q,r} A^T[i,p] * A^T[j,q] * A^T[k,r] * K[p,q,r] Since A^T[i,p] = a_p[i], this is indeed what we had. So the entire operation is a standard Conv3d with kernel W, stride=2, padding=0! And W has shape [16, 3, 3, 3, 3], same as K for the conv weights. Now, what about the bias? ConvTranspose3d can have a bias term. The reference doesn't explicitly set bias, and PyTorch default is bias=True for ConvTranspose3d. So there might be a bias. In the Model __init__, bias_shape is passed but not used. Wait, looking at the reference: self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding) PyTorch default for ConvTranspose3d is bias=True, so there IS a bias of shape [16]. Then batch norm follows. In eval mode, BN is: (x - running_mean) / sqrt(running_var + eps) * weight + bias. In train mode, it computes batch statistics. If we convert to a Conv3d, we'd need to account for the ConvTranspose3d bias and the BN parameters. Actually, this is getting quite complex. Let me verify my derivation numerically with a small example before committing to this approach. Given the complexity, let me think about whether there's a simpler approach that still gives good speedup. Simpler approach: 1. Implement a fused kernel for the sequence conv_transpose_output -> BN(if eval) -> avg_pool -> avg_pool 2. Or, use PyTorch for conv_transpose, then a custom kernel for BN + pools The large intermediate is [64, 16, 63, 63, 63] ≈ 256M floats ≈ 1GB. If I do conv_transpose via PyTorch (which uses cuDNN and is efficient), the output is in GPU memory. Then my custom kernel reads this 1GB and writes the final output [64, 16, 15, 15, 15] ≈ 3.2M floats. So memory bandwidth for the custom kernel: ~1GB read + 13MB write ≈ 1GB total. At 3.35 TB/s, this is ~0.3ms. Plus the conv_transpose time. In the reference, we have: 1. conv_transpose: reads input (3.1MB for [64,3,32,32,32]) + writes 1GB intermediate + lots of computation 2. batch_norm: reads 1GB + writes 1GB (or in-place) 3. avg_pool1: reads 1GB + writes ~16MB (for [64,16,31,31,31]) 4. avg_pool2: reads ~16MB + writes ~3.2MB Total memory traffic: input 3MB + intermediate 1GB + BN 1GB + pool1 read 1GB + pool1 write 16MB + pool2 read 16MB + output 3.2MB ≈ 3GB of memory traffic, plus computation. With my fused approach: 1. conv_transpose via PyTorch: ~1GB write + computation 2. custom fused kernel: read 1GB intermediate, write 3.2MB output, plus BN math and pooling Total: ~2GB + computation. Saves ~1GB of memory traffic. But actually, can I fuse conv_transpose with the pools? That would eliminate the 1GB intermediate entirely! If I write a custom conv_transpose kernel that directly computes the pooled output, I would: - Read input (3MB) + kernel weights (16*3*27 = 1296 floats, negligible) - Compute and write output (3.2MB) - No intermediate! This is the ideal case. But writing an efficient custom conv_transpose kernel from scratch is quite involved. However, since I derived that the whole sequence might be equivalent to a Conv3d, perhaps I can use PyTorch's conv3d which is highly optimized via cuDNN. But wait, is the equivalence exact? Let me think about the batch norm again. Actually, the key question is: can I use PyTorch's Conv3d to replace the ConvTranspose3d + 2x AvgPool sequence? If yes, then the optimized model becomes: 1. Conv3d with transformed weights, stride=2, padding=0 2. BatchNorm3d And that's it! This is much faster because: 1. Conv3d with stride=2 is much faster than ConvTranspose3d + pools 2. No large intermediate 3. Only 2 ops instead of 4 Plus, if in eval mode, I can fuse BN with Conv3d, getting just 1 op. Let me verify the equivalence more carefully. For a 1D example, let's say input = [x0, x1, x2, x3]. Transposed conv with kernel [k0, k1, k2], stride=2, padding=1: Upsampled: [x0, 0, x1, 0, x2, 0, x3, 0, ... wait, for 4 inputs, upsampled has 7 elements for stride 2: [x0, 0, x1, 0, x2, 0, x3] at indices 0,2,4,6. Conv with kernel=3, padding=1: y[0] = k0*0 + k1*x0 + k2*0 = k1*x0 y[1] = k0*x1 + k1*0 + k2*x0 = k0*x1 + k2*x0 y[2] = k0*0 + k1*x1 + k2*0 = k1*x1 y[3] = k0*x2 + k1*0 + k2*x1 = k0*x2 + k2*x1 y[4] = k0*0 + k1*x2 + k2*0 = k1*x2 y[5] = k0*x3 + k1*0 + k2*x2 = k0*x3 + k2*x2 y[6] = k0*0 + k1*x3 + k2*0 = k1*x3 y[7] = k0*0 + k1*0 + k2*x3 = k2*x3 (wait, with padding=1, we only go up to index 7? Actually for 7-element input with padding=1, output is 7+2-3+1=7. So indices 0..6.) Wait, upsampled length = 2*4-1 = 7. With padding=1, output = 7. So y[0..6]. But earlier I thought conv_transpose 32 -> 63. For input size 32, upsampled = 63. With padding=1, output = 63+2-3+1 = 63. Yes. For input size 4, upsampled = 7. Output = 7. Now avg_pool1 kernel=2, stride=2 on y[0..6]: z1[0] = (y[0]+y[1])/2 z1[1] = (y[2]+y[3])/2 z1[2] = (y[4]+y[5])/2 y[6] is dropped? 7 elements, pool size 2 stride 2: output = floor((7-2)/2)+1 = 3. So z1[0..2]. z1[0] = (k1*x0 + k0*x1 + k2*x0)/2 = ((k1+k2)*x0 + k0*x1)/2 z1[1] = (k1*x1 + k0*x2 + k2*x1)/2 = ((k1+k2)*x1 + k0*x2)/2 z1[2] = (k1*x2 + k0*x3 + k2*x2)/2 = ((k1+k2)*x2 + k0*x3)/2 Now avg_pool2 on z1[0..2] with size 2, stride 2: Output = floor((3-2)/2)+1 = 1. So z2[0] = (z1[0]+z1[1])/2. z2[0] = (((k1+k2)*x0 + k0*x1) + ((k1+k2)*x1 + k0*x2)) / 4 = ((k1+k2)*x0 + (k0+k1+k2)*x1 + k0*x2) / 4 By my formula, for j=0 with base 2j=0: depth offsets 0,1,2 correspond to x0, x1, x2. W[0] = (k1+k2)/4 W[1] = (k0+k1+k2)/4 W[2] = k0/4 Yes! z2[0] = W[0]*x0 + W[1]*x1 + W[2]*x2. And what would a strided conv with kernel [W0, W1, W2], stride=2, padding=0 give on input [x0, x1, x2, x3]? Output size = floor((4-3)/2)+1 = 1. Output[0] = W0*x0 + W1*x1 + W2*x2. Perfect match! So the equivalence is exact. Now let's think about the full 3D with batch and channels. For each output channel c_out and input channel c_in, we have a 3x3x3 kernel K[c_out, c_in, :, :, :]. The effective kernel W[c_out, c_in, i, j, k] = sum_{p,q,r} K[c_out, c_in, p,q,r] * a_p[i] * a_q[j] * a_r[k]. And the final output (before BN) is: y[n, c_out, j_d, j_h, j_w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{i,j,k=0..2} W[c_out, c_in, i,j,k] * x[n, c_in, 2*j_d+i, 2*j_h+j, 2*j_w+k] This is exactly a Conv3d with weights W, stride=2, padding=0! Now, about the bias. ConvTranspose3d has a bias vector b[c_out]. So the transposed conv output is: y_tc[d,h,w] = (sum over c_in of transposed_conv) + b[c_out] Then avg pools preserve the bias. Each pool averages, so after two pools: bias contribution = b[c_out] / 64? No wait. Actually, the bias is added to every element of the transposed conv output. After avg pool 1 (averaging 8 elements), each element gets b/8. After avg pool 2 (averaging 8 elements), each element gets (b/8)/8 = b/64? No, that's not right either. Wait. If every element of [63,63,63] has value b added, then the average of any 2x2x2 block is also b + (average of the conv part). The average of constant b over any region is just b. Then after the second avg pool, the average of constant b is still b. So the bias is preserved! It doesn't get divided. Let me verify: y_tc = conv_part + b. Pool1 output at some position = avg(conv_part over 2x2x2) + avg(b over 2x2x2) = avg(conv_part) + b. Pool2 output = avg(avg(conv_part) + b over 2x2x2) = avg(avg(conv_part)) + b. Yes, the bias is preserved unchanged! So if the original ConvTranspose3d has bias b[c_out], then after the two avg pools, the equivalent Conv3d should also have bias b[c_out]. Now, what about BatchNorm? In eval mode: BN(x) = (x - running_mean) / sqrt(running_var + eps) * gamma + beta. If we have Conv3d followed by BN, we can fuse them: fused_weight = weight * (gamma / sqrt(var + eps)) fused_bias = (bias - running_mean) * (gamma / sqrt(var + eps)) + beta Then Conv3d with fused_weight and fused_bias gives the same output. In train mode, it's more complex because BN computes statistics from the current batch. But wait, if we use PyTorch's nn.Conv3d + nn.BatchNorm3d in the reference-like structure, it would still do the BN separately. Let me define the transformation matrix A^T: A_t = torch.tensor([ [0.0, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.0, 0.0] ]) Wait, earlier I had: A^T = [[0, 1/4, 1/4], [1/4, 1/4, 0 ], [1/4, 0, 0 ]] Wait, let me recompute. A[p,i] = a_p[i]. a_0 = [0, 1/4, 1/4] a_1 = [1/4, 1/4, 0] a_2 = [1/4, 1/4, 0] So A^T[i,p] = A[p,i]: A^T[0] = [A[0,0], A[1,0], A[2,0]] = [0, 1/4, 1/4] A^T[1] = [A[0,1], A[1,1], A[2,1]] = [1/4, 1/4, 1/4] A^T[2] = [A[0,2], A[1,2], A[2,2]] = [1/4, 0, 0] So A^T = torch.tensor([ [0.0, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.0, 0.0] ]) Hmm wait, A^T[1] = [1/4, 1/4, 1/4], but A[2,1] = 1/4, yes. A^T[2] = [1/4, 0, 0], but A[0,2] = 1/4, A[1,2] = 0, A[2,2] = 0. Yes. So W[i,j,k] = sum_{p,q,r} K[p,q,r] * A^T[i,p] * A^T[j,q] * A^T[k,r] In PyTorch, I can compute this using einsum: W = torch.einsum('pqr, ip, jq, kr -> ijk', K, A_t, A_t, A_t) Wait, the ordering: W[i,j,k] = sum_p A_t[i,p] * sum_q A_t[j,q] * sum_r A_t[k,r] * K[p,q,r] So: W = torch.einsum('pqr,ip,jq,kr->ijk', K, A_t, A_t, A_t) And for all channels: W_full[c_out,c_in] = einsum('pqr,ip,jq,kr->ijk', K[c_in,c_out], A_t, A_t, A_t) Wait, ConvTranspose3d weight is [in_channels=3, out_channels=16, 3, 3, 3]. We need Conv3d weight [out_channels=16, in_channels=3, 3, 3, 3]. For each c_in in 0..2, c_out in 0..15: W[c_out, c_in] = einsum('pqr,ip,jq,kr->ijk', K_transpose[c_in, c_out], A_t, A_t, A_t) Now, what about bias? As I showed, the bias is preserved. Let me write the kernel: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() # Create temporary conv_transpose to get initialized weights temp_conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding) # Create equivalent conv3d self.conv3d = nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=0, bias=True) # Transform weights K = temp_conv_transpose.weight # [in_channels, out_channels, 3, 3, 3] A_t = torch.tensor([ [0.0, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.0, 0.0] ], dtype=K.dtype, device=K.device) with torch.no_grad(): for c_in in range(in_channels): for c_out in range(out_channels): k = K[c_in, c_out] # [3, 3, 3] w = torch.einsum('pqr,ip,jq,kr->ijk', k, A_t, A_t, A_t) self.conv3d.weight[c_out, c_in] = w if temp_conv_transpose.bias is not None: self.conv3d.bias.copy_(temp_conv_transpose.bias) else: self.conv3d.bias.zero_() self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm3d(out_channels) def forward(self, x): x = self.conv3d(x) x = self.batch_norm(x) return x ``` Wait, there's a problem. Let me design ModelNew like this: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape): super().__init__() # Keep same attributes for compatibility self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding) self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm3d(out_channels) self.avg_pool1 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2) self.avg_pool2 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2) # Precompute equivalent conv3d weights for use in forward self.register_buffer('conv3d_weight', torch.empty(out_channels, in_channels, 3, 3, 3)) self.register_buffer('conv3d_bias', torch.empty(out_channels)) self._update_conv3d_weights() # Register hook to update weights if conv_transpose changes self.conv_transpose.weight.register_hook(lambda grad: self._update_conv3d_weights()) def _update_conv3d_weights(self): K = self.conv_transpose.weight # [3, 16, 3, 3, 3] A_t = torch.tensor([...], device=K.device, dtype=K.dtype) for c_in in range(K.shape[0]): for c_out in range(K.shape[1]): k = K[c_in, c_out] w = torch.einsum('pqr,ip,jq,kr->ijk', k, A_t, A_t, A_t) self.conv3d_weight[c_out, c_in] = w if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None: self.conv3d_bias.copy_(self.conv_transpose.bias) else: self.conv3d_bias.zero_() ``` But hooks on parameters might be complicated. Actually, for a benchmark, weights are typically fixed after initialization. If the framework copies weights using load_state_dict, my ModelNew receives the updated state dict for conv_transpose, but my buffers won't update. Let me think about how to handle this. What if I define ModelNew without extra buffers, and in forward(), I dynamically compute the equivalent operation? I could: 1. In forward(), call the reference operations (conv_transpose + pool1 + pool2) using PyTorch 2. But this is slow. Or, in forward(), I construct the equivalent Conv3d dynamically: ```python def forward(self, x): K = self.conv_transpose.weight b = self.conv_transpose.bias A_t = ... W = torch.einsum('oipqr,ip,jq,kr->oijkr', K, A_t, A_t, A_t).permute(1, 0, 2, 3, 4) # But wait, K shape is [in, out, 3, 3, 3], need [out, in, 3, 3, 3] W = torch.einsum('iorst,ip,jq,kr->orijst', K, A_t, A_t, A_t) out = torch.nn.functional.conv3d(x, W, b, stride=2, padding=0) out = self.batch_norm(out) return out ``` This computes W on every forward pass. The einsum is on a small tensor (3*16*27 = 1296 elements), so it's very fast (microseconds) compared to the conv3d. This is a clean solution! It doesn't require any precomputed buffers or worrying about state dict loading. It just uses the same conv_transpose and batch_norm modules as the reference. Let me verify the einsum: K: [in_channels=3, out_channels=16, 3, 3, 3] A_t: [3, 3] W[out, in, i, j, k] = sum_{p,q,r} K[in, out, p, q, r] * A_t[i, p] * A_t[j, q] * A_t[k, r] In einsum: 'iopqr,ip,jq,kr->oijk' where i is in, o is out, p,q,r are kernel dims. Wait, einsum convention: output indices are o, i, j_indices, k_indices. In CUDA, I can implement this as: ```cuda __global__ void conv3d_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int in_D, int in_H, int in_W, int out_D, int out_H, int out_W ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C_out * out_D * out_H * out_W; if (idx >= total) return; // Decode idx to n, c_out, d, h, w int w = idx % out_W; int tmp = idx / out_W; int h = tmp % out_H; tmp /= out_H; int d = tmp % out_D; tmp /= out_D; int c_out = tmp % C_out; int n = tmp / C_out; float sum = bias[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; ++kd) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; ++kw) { int id = 2*d + kd; int ih = 2*h + kh; int iw = 2*w + kw; float in_val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * in_D + id) * in_H * in_W + ih * in_W + iw]; float w_val = weight[((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + kd) * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } output[idx] = sum; } ``` This is very naive but should work. However, the memory access pattern for weights is not great - each thread reads the same weight 256 times within a warp. But weights are small (5KB) and should fit in L1 cache. The input access pattern: adjacent threads in a warp process adjacent w coordinates, which means they access adjacent memory locations in width. With stride=2, adjacent threads access iw = 2*w + kw, which are also adjacent (for fixed kw). So memory access is coalesced. Actually, adjacent threads have consecutive idx values. Let's say thread 0 computes (n, c, d, h, w=0), thread 1 computes (n, c, d, h, w=1). Their input memory accesses are_offset by in_H*in_W elements in the width dimension. Since width=32, adjacent threads access memory 32 floats apart. Actually, in memory layout [N, C, D, H, W], the W dimension is contiguous. So adjacent w values are adjacent in memory. For threads with w=0 and w=1, they access memory locations that differ by 1 element (for the same c_in, d, h, kd, kh, kw). This is perfectly coalesced! Wait, let me be more precise. For a given (n, c_in, id, ih, iw), the memory location is: (((n * C_in + c_in) * in_D + id) * in_H + ih) * in_W + iw For two threads in the same warp with the same n, c_out, d, h but w differing by 1: thread w: iw = 2*w + kw, so iw differs by 2. So memory addresses differ by 2 floats. This is still coalesced (adjacent threads access adjacent or near-adjacent memory). For threads that differ in h: h differs by 1, so ih differs by 2, memory addresses differ by 2*in_W = 64 floats. Since a cache line is ~128 bytes = 32 floats, these might be in different cache lines, but memory access is still somewhat coalesced within each subgroup. With 256 threads per block, they might span multiple h values. Within a warp (32 threads), if we're iterating over w first, then 32 consecutive w values span 32/15 = 2 h values approximately. So threads in a warp might have w=0..14 and h=0 for the first 15, then w=0..14 and h=1 for the next 15. Actually, since w wraps every 15 values, 32 consecutive threads with consecutive idx span about 2 h values and some d values. This could reduce memory coalescing. Naive kernel: - 3.4M output elements - Each element: 81 MACs + 1 bias add - Total MACs: 280M - Memory traffic: read input 25MB, write output 14MB, read weights (cached) ~negligible - Total memory: ~40MB H100 memory bandwidth: 3.35 TB/s, so 40MB takes ~12 microseconds. H100 FP32 compute: 67 TFLOPS, so 280M FLOPs takes ~4 microseconds. If the kernel achieves 50% memory bandwidth and 30% compute, the time is bounded by compute at ~13 microseconds. For 100% memory bandwidth, ~12 microseconds. So the kernel should take around 10-15 microseconds. But the reference takes much longer. ConvTranspose3d on [64,3,32,32,32] to [64,16,63,63,63] is probably several milliseconds because it's a huge operation with large intermediate. And then the two pools add more time. So even a naive kernel should give massive speedup. Let me aim for a simple but correct kernel first. If I have time, I can optimize further. Now, about the CUDA kernel code and load_inline. Let me write the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_conv_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int in_D, int in_H, int in_W, int out_D, int out_H, int out_W ) { const int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int total = N * C_out * out_D * out_H * out_W; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % out_W; int t = idx / out_W; int h = t % out_H; t /= out_H; int d = t % out_D; t /= out_D; int c_out = t % C_out; int n = t / C_out; float sum = bias[c_out]; const int in_plane_size = in_H * in_W; const int in_channel_size = in_D * in_plane_size; const int in_batch_size = C_in * in_channel_size; const int out_plane_size = out_H * out_W; const int out_channel_size = out_D * out_plane_size; const int out_batch_size = C_out * out_channel_size; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float w0 = weight[((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + 0) * 9 + 0]; // ... One issue: with the nested loop, each thread computes a small number of MACs (81), which might cause warp divergence if different threads take different paths. For the weight transformation in Python: ```python def transform_weights(conv_transpose_weight): # conv_transpose_weight: [in_channels, out_channels, 3, 3, 3] A_t = torch.tensor([ [0.0, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.25, 0.25], [0.25, 0.0, 0.0] ], dtype=conv_transpose_weight.dtype, device=conv_transpose_weight.device) W = torch.einsum('copqr,ip,jq,kr->oijk', conv_transpose_weight, A_t, A_t, A_t) return W ``` And if bias exists, it's just copied. Wait, is the output of my conv3d exactly equal to the output of conv_transpose + 2 pools? I've shown they're mathematically equivalent, but do they give the exact same floating point result? Actually, there's another subtlety. In the reference, ConvTranspose3d might output [64, 16, 63, 63, 63] and then AvgPool3d(2, stride=2) is applied. For AvgPool3d with kernel_size=2 and stride=2, PyTorch computes sum / (2^3) = sum / 8. In my transformation, the factor 1/8 * 1/8 = 1/64 is incorporated into the kernel weights via the A_t matrix. Specifically, A_t has entries like 1/4, which represent the average over 4 elements in one dimension. Across 3 dimensions, A_t[i,p] * A_t[j,q] * A_t[k,r] incorporates the 1/64 factor. Wait, let me double-check this. In my earlier derivation: A_d(j, p) = sum_{d=4j}^{4j+3} u[d-p+1] / 4 Each A is divided by 4. The product of three A's gives division by 64. So yes, the 1/64 factor is naturally incorporated. And PyTorch's AvgPool3d divides by 8 each time, so total division by 64. Yes. So mathematically, my transformed weights produce exactly the same result (up to floating point rounding from different operation orderings). One more check: does PyTorch's AvgPool3d use exact division by 8, or does it compute the sum and then divide by the number of elements? For kernel_size=2, stride=2, it's always 8 elements, so it's sum/8. In PyTorch source, AvgPool3d uses `count_include_pad=True` by default. Since there's no padding, it's just sum / 8. And the two consecutive pools give sum(sum(x over 2x2x2) / 8) / 8 = sum(x over 4x4x4) / 64. Correct. Now I'm quite confident the equivalence holds. Let me write the full ModelNew code. Actually, thinking more about the grid layout: with idx as a linear index and decoding to (n, c_out, d, h, w), threads in a warp will have sequential w values, then h values, then d values, etc. When they load input, for the first iteration of the loop (c_in=0, kd=0, kh=0, kw=0), they access input at iw = 2*w + 0. Since w is sequential in the warp, iw is sequential (every other element). Memory access is coalesced. For different c_in values, they access different channels, which are offset by in_channel_size. This might cause some divergence in memory access, but all threads in a warp access the same c_in in the outer loop iteration, so they access the same channel. For a given warp, all threads access the same (n, c_in, kd, kh, kw) but different (d, h, w) positions. So memory access is coalesced within each iteration. The weights: all threads in the warp access the same weight W[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] for a given (c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw). But different threads might have different c_out! This is a problem. In my linear indexing, w varies fastest, then h, then d, then c_out, then n. So within a block of 256 threads, the first 15 threads might have c_out=0, the next 15 c_out=0 (with different d), etc. Actually: For idx in [0, 225] (225 = 15*15): these all have c_out=0, n=0, with varying d,h,w. When they read weights, threads 0-6 read from W[0,...] and threads 7-31 read from W[1,...]. This means divergent memory accesses within the warp! For output position (n, c_out, j_d, j_h, j_w), the final pooled value is the average over 4x4x4 conv_transpose output positions. Each conv_transpose output position depends on specific input positions. Instead of transforming weights, I can directly compute: sum = 0 for dd in 0..3: for dh in 0..3: for dw in 0..3: d = 4*j_d + dd h = 4*j_h + dh w = 4*j_w + dw # compute conv_transpose at (d, h, w) and add to sum sum = sum / 64 But computing conv_transpose at each position involves summing over input channels and kernel offsets. In the direct approach, I'm doing the averaging explicitly by iterating over 64 positions and dividing by 64. In the transformed approach, I absorbed the averaging into the weights, so I only do 81 ops. If I do the direct approach in the kernel, I'd need 5184 MACs per output instead of 81. Here's my plan for the kernel: ```cpp __global__ void fused_conv_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int in_D, int in_H, int in_W, int out_D, int out_H, int out_W ) { const int spatial = out_D * out_H * out_W; const int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int total = N * C_out * spatial; if (idx >= total) return; const int c_out = idx % C_out; const int t = idx / C_out; const int n = t / spatial; const int s = t % spatial; const int w = s % out_W; const int temp = s / out_W; const int h = temp % out_H; const int d = temp / out_H; const int in_plane = in_H * in_W; const int in_ch_stride = in_D * in_plane; const int in_batch_stride = C_in * in_ch_stride; const float* input_batch = input + n * in_batch_stride; float sum = bias[c_out]; #pragma unroll for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { const float* input_channel = input_batch + c_in * in_ch_stride; const float* weight_channel = weight + ((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 27); #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; ++kd) { int id = 2 * d + kd; const float* input_d = input_channel + id * in_plane; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; ++kh) { int ih = 2 * h + kh; const float* input_h = input_d + ih * in_W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; ++kw) { int iw = 2 * w + kw; float in_val = input_h[iw]; float w_val = weight_channel[kd * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } output[idx] = sum; } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the output indexing. With c_out varying fastest, the output layout is not standard PyTorch layout. In standard PyTorch, output is [N, C_out, D, H, W], which means C_out varies slower than spatial. But in my kernel, I'm writing output with c_out varying fastest. This is a different memory layout! I need to fix this. The output tensor should have standard PyTorch layout [N, C_out, D, H, W]. So if I want c_out to vary fastest in my thread indexing for better coalescing, I need to write to non-contiguous memory locations... OR I can accept non-standard layout and transpose afterwards. But that adds overhead. Let me think about this more carefully. Standard PyTorch layout for 5D: N, C, D, H, W. The stride for W is 1, H is W, D is H*W, C is D*H*W, N is C*D*H*W. In my indexing with c_out fastest, consecutive threads have consecutive c_out but the same spatial. Memory addresses differ by D*H*W. For out_D=15, out_H=15, out_W=15, spatial=3375. So consecutive c_out values are 3375 floats apart. If a warp has threads with c_out=0..15 for spatial=0, they access memory at addresses 0, 3375, 6750, ... This is strided access with stride 3375. Since 3375 * 4 bytes = 13500 bytes, and cache lines are ~128 bytes, each thread accesses a different cache line. This is the WORST possible memory access pattern! So having c_out vary fastest in threads is terrible for output write coalescing. To have coalesced output writes, I need adjacent threads to write to adjacent memory locations. In standard PyTorch layout, adjacent memory locations correspond to adjacent W values. So the natural layout where w varies fastest in thread indexing is actually best for output coalescing. Earlier I was worried about weight access. Let me reconsider. If threads have sequential w values, they write to consecutive memory locations. Good. When reading weights, if threads have different c_out, they access different weight channels. If c_out differs, weight addresses are separated by C_in*27 elements. For C_in=3, that's 81 floats apart. Since all threads in a warp might span multiple c_out values, weight access is strided. But weights are only 5KB! The entire weight tensor fits in a few cache lines. More precisely, 1296 floats * 4 bytes = 5184 bytes ≈ 41 cache lines of 128 bytes each. With L1 cache of ~128KB per SM, this easily fits. And since all threads access the same small weight tensor repeatedly, it will be very well cached. So weight access is not a bottleneck even if it's not perfectly coalesced. But output writes should be coalesced for good performance. Therefore, I should stick with the natural layout: w varies fastest, then h, then d, then c_out, then n. Let me go back to the original indexing: idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x w = idx % out_W h = (idx / out_W) % out_H d = (idx / (out_W * out_H)) % out_D c_out = (idx / spatial) % C_out n = idx / (spatial * C_out) This gives coalesced output writes. For weight access: all threads in a warp might have the same c_out if spatial_size * warp_size fits within one c_out's worth of indices. Warp size is 32. spatial_size=3375. So 32 consecutive threads all have the same c_out (since 32 < 3375). Great! They all access the same weight element W[c_out, c_in, ...] for a given (c_in, kd, kh, kw). This means all threads in a warp access the same weight address, which is a broadcast. Perfect! And for input access: adjacent threads have adjacent w values, so iw = 2*w + kw is also adjacent (consecutive even or odd numbers depending on kw). Input memory for the same (n, c_in, d, h, kd, kh) but varying w differs by 1 element in memory (for the same kw). So memory access is coalesced. Actually, let me verify: Thread 0: w=0, iw=0 (for kw=0) Thread 1: w=1, iw=2 (for kw=0) Thread 2: w=2, iw=4 (for kw=0) Memory addresses: input_base + 0, input_base + 2, input_base + 4, ... These are strided by 2. Each address differs by 8 bytes. A cache line is 128 bytes (32 floats). So threads 0-15 access memory within 15*8 = 120 bytes, which is almost one cache line. This is still coalesced into a few transactions. For kw=1: Thread 0: iw=1 Thread 1: iw=3 Memory addresses: input_base + 4, input_base + 12, ... (4 bytes per float, so difference of 8 bytes). Again, within one cache line. So input access is very well coalesced. For different h values in the warp: A warp of 32 threads with w=0..14, h=0 for threads 0-14, and w=0..14, h=1 for threads 15-29, thread 30: w=0,h=2, thread 31: w=1,h=2. For threads 0-14 and 15-29, h differs by 1. So ih differs by 2. Memory address differs by 2*in_W = 64 floats = 256 bytes. This is 2 cache lines. The first 15 threads access one set of cache lines, the next 15 access another set. This is still efficient. For threads 30-31, h=2, addresses differ by another 2*in_W = 256 bytes from h=1. So within a warp, there are 3 groups accessing 3 different h levels. Each group has coalesced access. The GPU can handle this with multiple memory transactions per warp. This is actually quite good. And for output writes: the same pattern applies. Threads 0-14 write consecutive w for h=0, threads 15-29 write consecutive w for h=1. These are separated by out_W=15 floats = 60 bytes! Wait, output address for (c_out, d, h, w) is ((c_out * out_D + d) * out_H + h) * out_W + w. For thread 0: (c_out, d, h=0, w=0) -> addr = c_out*spatial + d*out_H*out_W + 0*out_W + 0 = base For thread 1: (c_out, d, h=0, w=1) -> addr = base + 1 ... For thread 15: (c_out, d, h=1, w=0) -> addr = base + out_W = base + 15 Since out_W=15, threads 0-14 write to base + 0..14, threads 15-29 write to base + 15..29. These are all consecutive! A warp of 32 threads (except threads 30-31 which go to base+30..31) writes to 32 consecutive memory locations. This is PERFECTLY coalesced! Wait, I need to check: idx=0: n=0,c=0,d=0,h=0,w=0 -> spatial=0 -> addr=0 idx=1: n=0,c=0,d=0,h=0,w=1 -> spatial=1 -> addr=1 ... idx=14: n=0,c=0,d=0,h=0,w=14 -> spatial=14 -> addr=14 idx=15: n=0,c=0,d=0,h=1,w=0 -> spatial=15 -> addr=15 ... idx=29: n=0,c=0,d=0,h=1,w=14 -> spatial=29 -> addr=29 idx=30: n=0,c=0,d=0,h=2,w=0 -> spatial=30 -> addr=30 idx=31: n=0,c=0,d=0,h=2,w=1 -> spatial=31 -> addr=31 All 32 threads write to addresses 0..31. Perfect coalescing! When we reach idx=3375: c_out changes from 0 to 1. addr=3375. The next warp writes to 3375..3406, which is also coalesced. Great! So the natural linear indexing with w varying fastest is actually optimal for both input and output coalescing. For weights: threads 0..31 all have c_out=0 (since 3375 > 31). They all access the same weight W[0, c_in, kd, kh, kw]. This is broadcast, which is as efficient as it gets. This is excellent. The naive kernel should be quite efficient due to good memory access patterns. Now let me write the final code. One optimization: I can compute the input and weight base pointers outside the loops to reduce address calculations. ```cpp __global__ void fused_conv_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int in_D, int in_H, int in_W, int out_D, int out_H, int out_W ) { const int spatial = out_D * out_H * out_W; const int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int total = N * C_out * spatial; if (idx >= total) return; const int w = idx % out_W; const int h = (idx / out_W) % out_H; const int d = (idx / (out_W * out_H)) % out_D; const int c_out = (idx / spatial) % C_out; const int n = idx / (C_out * spatial); const int in_plane = in_H * in_W; const int in_ch_stride = in_D * in_plane; const int in_batch_stride = C_in * in_ch_stride; const float* input_batch = input + n * in_batch_stride; const float* weight_cout = weight + c_out * C_in * 27; float sum = bias[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { const float* input_ch = input_batch + c_in * in_ch_stride; const float* weight_ch = weight_cout + c_in * 27; #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; ++kd) { int id = 2 * d + kd; const float* input_d = input_ch + id * in_plane; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; ++kh) { int ih = 2 * h + kh; const float* input_h = input_d + ih * in_W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; ++kw) { int iw = 2 * w + kw; sum += input_h[iw] * weight_ch[kd * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } } output[idx] = sum; } ``` I used `#pragma unroll` for the 3-element loops. The compiler should unroll these automatically since the loop bounds are small constants, but explicit unroll hints can't hurt. One concern: the innermost loop accesses `input_h[iw]` where `iw = 2*w + kw`. For consecutive threads (consecutive w), `iw` is also consecutive (for fixed kw). So threads in a warp access consecutive memory. This is coalesced. For `weight_ch[kd * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]`, all threads in the warp access the same memory address (same c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw). This is broadcast, very efficient. So the kernel should have excellent memory behavior. Now, for the host wrapper function: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor fused_conv_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias ) { int N = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int in_D = input.size(2); int in_H = input.size(3); int in_W = input.size(4); int C_out = weight.size(0); int out_D = (in_D - 3) / 2 + 1; int out_H = (in_H - 3) / 2 + 1; int out_W = (in_W - 3) / 2 + 1; auto output = torch::empty({N, C_out, out_D, out_H, out_W}, input.options()); int total = N * C_out * out_D * out_H * out_W; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; fused_conv_forward_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, in_D, in_H, in_W, out_D, out_H, out_W ); return output; } ``` And the load_inline call. ConvTranspose3d is actually a convolution with dilated input. For stride=2, padding=1: The transposed convolution can be written as: - Insert stride-1 zeros between input values - Pad with `kernel_size - padding - 1 = 3 - 1 - 1 = 1` on each side - Do a standard convolution But it might be simpler to implement the direct formula: For each output position (d, h, w), it receives contributions from input positions where: (d - kd + padding) % stride == 0, etc. Specifically, for ConvTranspose3d with stride s, padding p: out[d, h, w] = sum over valid input positions and kernel weights Actually, let me think about it differently. The standard way to think of transposed conv: For a standard conv with stride s, padding p, the transposed conv maps from output space to input space. A simpler approach: ConvTranspose3d with stride=2, padding=1, kernel=3 is equivalent to: 1. Upsample input by 2x (insert zeros) 2. Pad with 1 on each side 3. Convolve with the same weights (but flipped? no, PyTorch handles this) Actually in PyTorch, ConvTranspose3d uses the transposed of the convolution matrix. But we can also express it as: For output position (d_out, h_out, w_out): d_out = d_in * stride + kd - padding, but only when this is in valid range. Wait, the actual formula for transposed convolution: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, d', h', w'] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] Where: d' = (d + padding - kd) / stride, and this must be an integer in [0, in_D-1] h' = (h + padding - kh) / stride w' = (w + padding - kw) / stride So for each output position (d, h, w), we check which kernel positions (kd, kh, kw) give valid integer d', h', w'. Actually wait, let me recalculate: For d=0: (0+1-0)/2=0.5(invalid), (0+1-1)/2=0(valid), (0+1-2)/2=-0.5(invalid) For d=1: (1+1-0)/2=1(valid), (1+1-1)/2=0.5(invalid), (1+1-2)/2=0(valid) For d=2: (2+1-0)/2=1.5(invalid), (2+1-1)/2=1(valid), (2+1-2)/2=0.5(invalid) For d=3: (3+1-0)/2=2(valid), (3+1-1)/2=1.5(invalid), (3+1-2)/2=1(valid) So the pattern is more complex. Actually, a cleaner approach is to realize that transposed conv with stride=2 can be rewritten by rearranging the weights, and then doing a stride-1 conv on the upsampled input. But there's an even better way: For ConvTranspose3d(k=3, s=2, p=1), we can use the "sub-pixel" or "efficient" transposed conv formulation where we decompose it into a stride-1 conv on the input without upsampling, by appropriately rearranging weights. But this is non-trivial for 3D. Alternatively, I should just implement the direct formula carefully. Let me also consider that we can fuse batch_norm + avgpool1 + avgpool2. For the avg pools: - After conv_transpose: (N, 16, 63, 63, 63) - After AvgPool3d(2): (N, 16, 31, 31, 31) - After AvgPool3d(2): (N, 16, 15, 15, 15) AvgPool3d(2) on (63,63,63) → floor(63/2)=31 for each dim. AvgPool3d(2) on (31,31,31) → floor(31/2)=15 for each dim. Two consecutive AvgPool3d(2) is equivalent to a single AvgPool3d with kernel=4 and counting overlap correctly. Specifically, AvgPool3d(k=2) followed by AvgPool3d(k=2) on a spatial dimension is: First pool: y[i] = (x[2i] + x[2i+1]) / 2 Second pool: z[j] = (y[2j] + y[2j+1]) / 2 = (x[4j] + x[4j+1] + x[4j+2] + x[4j+3]) / 4 But wait, if we apply this to 63 elements: After first pool: indices 0-30 (31 elements) = average of pairs (0,1), (2,3), ..., (60,61). Note element 62 is dropped. After second pool: indices 0-14 (15 elements) = average of pairs from first pool. So the effective pooling is: For output position j in 0..14: z[j] = average of x[4j], x[4j+1], x[4j+2], x[4j+3] in that dimension. But we must be careful: this ignores x[60], x[61], x[62] since 63 is odd! Actually x[60] and x[61] are used in first pool index 30, and x[62] is dropped. Then first pool index 30 is dropped in second pool since 31 is odd. So effectively for output j, we average x[4j:4j+4] in each dimension. But actually, even simpler: the full computation is: 1. Compute ConvTranspose3d 2. Apply BN: y = gamma * (x - mean) / std + beta, where std = sqrt(var + eps) 3. AvgPool3d(2) twice So the final fused kernel can compute for each output pixel: 1. Sum over the 4x4x4 region in the conv_transpose output 2. Divide by 64 3. Apply BN Since conv_transpose is linear, we can write: final_output[n, c, d, h, w] = (1/64) * sum_{dd,dh,dw in 0..3} BN(conv_transpose(input)[4d+dd, 4h+dh, 4w+dw]) But BN is applied per-channel, so: = (1/64) * sum_{dd,dh,dw} [scale[c] * conv_transpose(input)[..., 4d+dd, 4h+dh, 4w+dw] + shift[c]] = scale[c] * (1/64) * sum conv_transpose(input) + shift[c] Wait, is that right? BN is y = (x - running_mean) / sqrt(running_var + eps) * weight + bias. So it's an affine transform: y = scale * x + shift where: scale = weight / sqrt(running_var + eps) shift = bias - weight * running_mean / sqrt(running_var + eps) So yes, BN is affine per channel: y[c] = scale[c] * x[c] + shift[c] And since avgpool is also linear: avgpool(BN(x)) = BN(avgpool(x)) if we think about it: avgpool(scale * x + shift) = scale * avgpool(x) + shift (because shift is constant over the pooling region) So: final_output = scale[c] * (avgpool(conv_transpose(input))) + shift[c] And avgpool(conv_transpose(input)) over 4x4x4 is just a sum divided by 64. Now, conv_transpose(input)[pos] is a linear function of input. So we can fold the entire thing: For each output position (d,h,w) and each output channel c: final_output = scale[c] * (1/64) * sum_{kd,kh,kw in 0..3} conv_transpose(input)[4d+kd, 4h+kh, 4w+kw] + shift[c] But conv_transpose(input)[D,H,W] = sum_{c_in, i,j,k where valid} input[c_in, d_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[c_in, c_out, i, j, k] So we need to know, for each output position (D=4d+kd, H=4h+kh, W=4w+kw) of conv_transpose, which input positions and kernel weights contribute. This is getting complex. Another approach: implement the conv_transpose correctly, then apply avgpool + bn directly in the kernel. Wait, even simpler: the conv_transpose output has shape (64, 16, 63, 63, 63) which is about 256M elements. That's quite large. The two avg pools reduce it to (64, 16, 15, 15, 15) ≈ 3.4M elements. So there's a huge reduction. If I can compute only the elements of conv_transpose that actually contribute to the final output, that would save a lot of computation. The elements that contribute are those at positions where none of the dimensions is 60, 61, or 62 (since those get dropped by the pooling). Actually: First AvgPool3d(2) on 63 elements: - Takes indices: [0,1], [2,3], ..., [58,59], [60,61], and drops [62] - Output has 31 elements: indices 0..30 correspond to input ranges [0:2], [2:4], ..., [60:62] Second AvgPool3d(2) on 31 elements: - Takes indices: [0,1], [2,3], ..., [28,29], and drops [30] - Output has 15 elements: indices 0..14 correspond to first-pool indices [0:2], [2:4], ..., [28:30] So final output index d corresponds to first-pool indices 2d and 2d+1, which correspond to conv_transpose indices: - First-pool index 2d: covers conv_transpose [4d, 4d+1] - First-pool index 2d+1: covers conv_transpose [4d+2, 4d+3] So for final output d (0-14), the conv_transpose indices used are [4d, 4d+1, 4d+2, 4d+3]! But conv_transpose naturally produces [0:63]! Actually, a cleaner mathematical expression: For output index o in final (0-14), the value is the average of conv_transpose over indices [4o, 4o+1, 4o+2, 4o+3]. So we can compute for each final output pixel, we sum over 4x4x4 = 64 positions in conv_transpose space, and each of those is a sum over C_in * 3 * 3 * 3 = 81 input values. So total operations per output pixel: 64 * 81 = 5184 multiplications. Total output pixels: 64 * 16 * 15 * 15 * 15 = 3,456,000 Total multiplications: ~17.9 billion. That's a lot. But actually, we can write this more efficiently. Notice that conv_transpose with stride=2, padding=1, kernel=3 can be written as: For each position in the output grid of conv_transpose, the formula is non-zero only for certain input positions. Let me look at the transposed conv formula from PyTorch: For ConvTranspose3d with stride=2, padding=1: The output at position (d, h, w) is: sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} input[c_in, d', h', w'] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] where d' = (d + padding - kd) / stride = (d + 1 - kd) / 2, and similarly for h', w'. For this to be valid, (d + 1 - kd) must be non-negative, even, and d' < in_D. For a fixed output position (d, h, w), the kernel indices (kd, kh, kw) that contribute are those where (d + 1 - kd) is even and non-negative and d' < in_D. Since in_D = 32, d ranges from 0 to 62. For d even: d + 1 is odd, so (d + 1 - kd) is even when kd is odd, i.e., kd = 1. For d odd: d + 1 is even, so (d + 1 - kd) is even when kd is even, i.e., kd = 0 or 2. And for d even, kd = 1: d' = d / 2, which is in [0, 31], valid. For d odd, kd = 0: d' = (d + 1) / 2, which for d in {1,3,...,61} gives d' in {1,2,...,31}, valid. For d odd, kd = 2: d' = (d - 1) / 2, which for d in {1,3,...,61} gives d' in {0,1,...,30}, valid. Wait, what about d=0 (even)? kd=1: d' = 0/2 = 0. Valid. d=62 (even)? kd=1: d' = 62/2 = 31. Valid. d=61 (odd)? kd=0: d' = 62/2 = 31. Valid. kd=2: d' = 60/2 = 30. Valid. So the pattern is: - Even d: only kd=1 contributes, d' = d/2 - Odd d: kd=0 contributes with d' = (d+1)/2, kd=2 contributes with d' = (d-1)/2 Each contributes 1 input position per kernel dimension. So per spatial dimension of output: - Even positions: 1 input position - Odd positions: 2 input positions Total valid (kd, kh, kw) combinations: - If d, h, w are all even: 1 * 1 * 1 = 1 term - If one is odd, others even: 2 * 1 * 1 = 2 terms - If two are odd: 2 * 2 * 1 = 4 terms - If all three are odd: 2 * 2 * 2 = 8 terms So it's much sparser than full 27 weights! And the weight indices used depend on (d % 2, h % 2, w % 2). Let me define lookup tables: For even d (d % 2 == 0): - Use kd_list = [1], d'_list = [d/2] For odd d (d % 2 == 1): - Use kd_list = [0, 2], d'_list = [(d+1)/2, (d-1)/2] This means for conv_transpose output, the number of multiplies varies from 1 to 8 times C_in per output pixel. Now for the final fused kernel, we need to average over 4x4x4 conv_transpose positions for each final output pixel. For a final output at (d_f, h_f, w_f), we need conv_transpose at: d_c in {4*d_f, 4*d_f+1, 4*d_f+2, 4*d_f+3} Let me look at the parity: - 4*d_f is always even - 4*d_f+1 is odd - 4*d_f+2 is even - 4*d_f+3 is odd So among the 4 d positions: 2 even, 2 odd. Same for h and w. For each (d_c, h_c, w_c) combination: - d_c even: 1 kd choice - d_c odd: 2 kd choices Total terms per (d_c, h_c, w_c): up to 8. Over 64 positions: average number of terms is (1+2+1+2)*(1+2+1+2)*(1+2+1+2) / 64 * 27? Actually, the distribution over the 64 positions: - Even-even-even positions (8 of them): each uses 1*1*1 = 1 weight-index combo - Even-even-odd (8 of them): 1*1*2 = 2 - Even-odd-even (8): 1*2*1 = 2 - Even-odd-odd (8): 1*2*2 = 4 - Odd-even-even (8): 2*1*1 = 2 - Odd-even-odd (8): 2*1*2 = 4 - Odd-odd-even (8): 2*2*1 = 4 - Odd-odd-odd (8): 2*2*2 = 8 Total weight-index combos over 64 positions: 8*(1+2+2+4+2+4+4+8) = 8*27 = 216. But wait, that's counting per spatial position. Each weight-index combo corresponds to a specific (kd, kh, kw). And for each, we also need the correct input channel. For the 64 conv_transpose positions, the total number of (kd, kh, kw, c_in) combinations is: Sum over the 64 positions of (number of valid kd) * (number of valid kh) * (number of valid kw) * C_in. And as we see from above, the sum is 216. So with C_in=3, there are 648 multiplications per output pixel. And when we fold everything, we can compute the final output as: For each output pixel and channel: sum over 64 conv_transpose positions, and for each, sum over the valid (kd, kh, kw, c_in): input[c_in, d_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] Where for each conv_transpose position (d_c = 4*d_f + dd, etc.), we compute d_in based on parity. But we can rewrite this to group by c_in and (kd, kh, kw): For each final output position (d_f, h_f, w_f) and channel c_out: We iterate over all possible (c_in, kd, kh, kw), and for each, we need to know which of the 64 conv_transpose positions it appears in. Wait, a given (kd, kh, kw) appears at a conv_transpose position (d_c, h_c, w_c) if and only if: - If kd = 0: d_c must be odd, and d_in = (d_c + 1) / 2 - If kd = 1: d_c must be even, and d_in = d_c / 2 - If kd = 2: d_c must be odd, and d_in = (d_c - 1) / 2 For a fixed (kd, kh, kw) and (d_f, h_f, w_f), the conv_transpose positions that include this kernel element are: d_c in {4*d_f, 4*d_f+1, 4*d_f+2, 4*d_f+3} with the right parity for kd. - For kd=0: d_c must be odd, so d_c ∈ {4*d_f+1, 4*d_f+3} d_in values: (4*d_f+1+1)/2 = 2*d_f+1, and (4*d_f+3+1)/2 = 2*d_f+2 - For kd=1: d_c must be even, so d_c ∈ {4*d_f, 4*d_f+2} d_in values: 4*d_f/2 = 2*d_f, and (4*d_f+2)/2 = 2*d_f+1 - For kd=2: d_c must be odd, so d_c ∈ {4*d_f+1, 4*d_f+3} d_in values: (4*d_f+1-1)/2 = 2*d_f, and (4*d_f+3-1)/2 = 2*d_f+1 So we see that for each kd, there are exactly 2 valid d_c positions in the 4x4x4 window, and they correspond to 2 consecutive d_in values. Similarly for h and w. So for a fixed (kd, kh, kw) and output position (d_f, h_f, w_f): - There are 2*2*2 = 8 conv_transpose positions in the 4x4x4 window that use this kernel element - These correspond to a 2x2x2 block of input positions Specifically: - kd=0: d_in ∈ {2*d_f+1, 2*d_f+2} - kd=1: d_in ∈ {2*d_f, 2*d_f+1} - kd=2: d_in ∈ {2*d_f, 2*d_f+1} So the total contribution of (c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw) to the final output is: weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] * sum over the 2x2x2 input block And the sum over the 2x2x2 input block depends on (kd, kh, kw) and (d_f, h_f, w_f). For example, for (kd=0, kh=0, kw=0): d_in ∈ {2*d_f+1, 2*d_f+2} h_in ∈ {2*h_f+1, 2*h_f+2} w_in ∈ {2*w_f+1, 2*w_f+2} Sum = sum of input[c_in, 2*d_f+1:2*d_f+3, 2*h_f+1:2*h_f+3, 2*w_f+1:2*w_f+3] For (kd=1, kh=1, kw=1): d_in ∈ {2*d_f, 2*d_f+1} h_in ∈ {2*h_f, 2*h_f+1} w_in ∈ {2*w_f, 2*w_f+1} Sum = sum of input[c_in, 2*d_f:2*d_f+2, 2*h_f:2*h_f+2, 2*w_f:2*w_f+2] This is actually a very nice structure! The 27 kernel elements can be grouped, and each corresponds to a sum over a 2x2x2 sub-block of the input, where the sub-block is shifted based on the kernel index. Specifically, the input sub-block for (kd, kh, kw) is: d_start = (kd == 1 || kd == 2) ? 2*d_f : 2*d_f+1 Wait, for kd=0: {2*d_f+1, 2*d_f+2} → start = 2*d_f+1 kd=1: {2*d_f, 2*d_f+1} → start = 2*d_f kd=2: {2*d_f, 2*d_f+1} → start = 2*d_f So: - kd=0: d_start = 2*d_f+1 - kd=1 or 2: d_start = 2*d_f Similarly: - kh=0: h_start = 2*h_f+1 - kh=1 or 2: h_start = 2*h_f - kw=0: w_start = 2*w_f+1 - kw=1 or 2: w_start = 2*w_f Now, we can precompute for each output position the 27 sums of 2x2x2 input blocks, then dot with the 27 weights per channel. Actually, there are only 2 choices per dimension for the start position, so there are at most 8 unique 2x2x2 sub-blocks. But the 27 kernel elements map to some of these 8 sub-blocks. Let me see: d_start can be 2*d_f or 2*d_f+1 (2 choices) h_start can be 2*h_f or 2*h_f+1 w_start can be 2*w_f or 2*w_f+1 So 8 possible sub-blocks. When we sum over the 2x2x2 block starting at (d_s, h_s, w_s), let's call this sum S[d_s%2][h_s%2][w_s%2] (where %2 here means whether it's the lower or upper option). Actually, let's index by (kd, kh, kw): S_kd_kh_kw = sum of input over d_in in [start_d, start_d+1], etc. For kd ∈ {0,1,2}: start_d = 2*d_f + (kd == 0 ? 1 : 0) So S_kd_kh_kw is the sum over input[c_in, start_d:start_d+2, start_h:start_h+2, start_w:start_w+2]. Since there are only 2 unique start_d values (for kd=0 and kd∈{1,2}), and similarly for h and w, there are only 8 unique S values. But computing 8 sums of 2x2x2 blocks for each c_in at each output position is: 8 * 8 = 64 loads per c_in (each 2x2x2 sum needs 8 loads, but adjacent sums share elements). Actually, for a fixed output position (d_f, h_f, w_f), the input region accessed is: d_in ∈ [2*d_f, 2*d_f+2] for kd∈{1,2} and [2*d_f+1, 2*d_f+2] for kd=0. So overall d_in ∈ [2*d_f, 2*d_f+2] (3 values). Similarly h_in ∈ [2*h_f, 2*h_f+2] and w_in ∈ [2*w_f, 2*w_f+2]. So we need the 3x3x3 input block centered-ish at (2*d_f, 2*h_f, 2*w_f). For each of the C_in=3 channels, we can load this 3x3x3 region and compute the 8 possible 2x2x2 sums. For d_f=0, we need d_in ∈ [0, 2]. For d_f=14, we need d_in ∈ [28, 30]. Since in_D=32, d_in=30 is valid (index 30 and 31 for the 2x2x2 sum). So all within bounds! Similarly for h and w. So the total algorithm per thread: For a given output position (n, c_out, d_f, h_f, w_f): 1. Compute the 8 sums of 2x2x2 input blocks from the 3x3x3 region around (2*d_f, 2*h_f, 2*w_f) 2. For each c_in and each (kd, kh, kw), dot the appropriate sum with weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] 3. Sum all contributions, divide by 64, apply BN scale and shift Wait, actually the sum over the 2x2x2 block appears exactly once for each valid conv_transpose position. But we established there are exactly 8 conv_transpose positions that use a given (kd, kh, kw), and each corresponds to a sum over the same 2x2x2 block. That means the total contribution of weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] to the final output is: weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] * sum_of_2x2x2_block And there are 27 such terms. So we need to compute 27 products and sum them. But the 27 sums of 2x2x2 blocks fall into 8 categories. So we can compute 8 2x2x2 sums, then use each sum for multiple (kd, kh, kw) combinations. For example: kd=0: d_start = 2*d_f+1 kd=1: d_start = 2*d_f kd=2: d_start = 2*d_f So kd=1 and kd=2 use the SAME 2x2x2 block in the d dimension. That means for (kd=1, kh=0, kw=0) and (kd=2, kh=0, kw=0), the d dimension sum is the same (d_in from 2*d_f to 2*d_f+1), but h and w differ. Actually, the 2x2x2 blocks are indexed by their start positions: d_start can be 2*d_f or 2*d_f+1 h_start can be 2*h_f or 2*h_f+1 w_start can be 2*w_f or 2*w_f+1 - (kd=0, kh=0, kw=0) → (2*d_f+1, 2*h_f+1, 2*w_f+1) - (kd=0, kh=0, kw=1) → (2*d_f+1, 2*h_f+1, 2*w_f) - (kd=0, kh=0, kw=2) → (2*d_f+1, 2*h_f+1, 2*w_f) - (kd=0, kh=1, kw=0) → (2*d_f+1, 2*h_f, 2*w_f+1) - etc. So indeed, (kd=1, kh, kw) and (kd=2, kh, kw) always use the same 2x2x2 block! For any fixed (kh, kw), the block for kd=1 equals the block for kd=2. Similarly: - kh=1 and kh=2 use the same block - kw=1 and kw=2 use the same block So there are only 2 unique choices per dimension, giving 8 unique blocks total. But some (kd, kh, kw) combinations map to the same block: - kd ∈ {1,2}, kh ∈ {1,2}, kw ∈ {1,2} → 2*2*2 = 8 combinations map to 1 block - kd=0, kh ∈ {1,2}, kw ∈ {1,2} → 1*2*2 = 4 combinations map to another block - kd ∈ {1,2}, kh=0, kw ∈ {1,2} → 2*1*2 = 4 combinations map to another - kd=0, kh=0, kw ∈ {1,2} → 1*1*2 = 2 combinations - kd ∈ {1,2}, kh ∈ {1,2}, kw=0 → 2*2*1 = 4 combinations - kd=0, kh ∈ {1,2}, kw=0 → 1*2*1 = 2 combinations - kd ∈ {1,2}, kh=0, kw=0 → 2*1*1 = 2 combinations - kd=0, kh=0, kw=0 → 1 combination Total: 8 + 4 + 4 + 2 + 4 + 2 + 2 + 1 = 27. Good. So if we precompute 8 2x2x2 sums, then the total contribution is: sum over 8 blocks of (sum of weights mapping to that block) * (block sum) Let me define: W_block[0] = sum of weights with kd=0, kh=0, kw=0 W_block[1] = sum of weights with kd=0, kh=0, kw∈{1,2} W_block[2] = sum of weights with kd=0, kh∈{1,2}, kw=0 W_block[3] = sum of weights with kd=0, kh∈{1,2}, kw∈{1,2} W_block[4] = sum of weights with kd∈{1,2}, kh=0, kw=0 W_block[5] = sum of weights with kd∈{1,2}, kh=0, kw∈{1,2} W_block[6] = sum of weights with kd∈{1,2}, kh∈{1,2}, kw=0 W_block[7] = sum of weights with kd∈{1,2}, kh∈{1,2}, kw∈{1,2} And the corresponding block sums: S[0] = sum over [2d_f+1:2d_f+3, 2h_f+1:2h_f+3, 2w_f+1:2w_f+3] S[1] = sum over [2d_f+1:2d_f+3, 2h_f+1:2h_f+3, 2w_f:2w_f+2] S[2] = sum over [2d_f+1:2d_f+3, 2h_f:2h_f+2, 2w_f+1:2w_f+3] S[3] = sum over [2d_f+1:2d_f+3, 2h_f:2h_f+2, 2w_f:2w_f+2] S[4] = sum over [2d_f:2d_f+2, 2h_f+1:2h_f+3, 2w_f+1:2w_f+3] S[5] = sum over [2d_f:2d_f+2, 2h_f+1:2h_f+3, 2w_f:2w_f+2] S[6] = sum over [2d_f:2d_f+2, 2h_f:2h_f+2, 2w_f+1:2w_f+3] S[7] = sum over [2d_f:2d_f+2, 2h_f:2h_f+2, 2w_f:2w_f+2] Then the conv_transpose(output region) before pooling and BN is: sum_{i=0}^7 W_block[i] * S[i] This requires only 8 multiplies per input channel, instead of 27! And then we divide by 64 for the average pool, and apply BN: final = scale * (sum/64) + shift. But wait, is this correct? Let me verify. The same (kd, kh, kw) appears at 8 different conv_transpose positions, but each time with a DIFFERENT input position! The input position depends on the exact conv_transpose position. For example, for (kd=0, kh=0, kw=0): At conv_transpose position d_c = 4*d_f + 1: input d' = (4*d_f+1+1)/2 = 2*d_f+1 At conv_transpose position d_c = 4*d_f + 3: input d' = (4*d_f+3+1)/2 = 2*d_f+2 So yes, the input d' values are 2*d_f+1 and 2*d_f+2. Now, for a single output pixel in the final output, we need to compute: sum_{c_in} sum_{i=0}^7 W_block[c_in, c_out, i] * S[c_in, i] / 64 Then apply BN: scale[c_out] * result + shift[c_out] Where W_block[c_in, c_out, i] = sum of weights[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] for all (kd, kh, kw) that map to block i. These W_block values are constants per channel pair and can be precomputed once. So the per-thread computation: 1. For a given output position, the 8 block sums can be computed from the 3x3x3 input region. Instead of computing each 2x2x2 sum independently (which would be 8 * 8 = 64 loads), we can reuse loads. The 8 2x2x2 blocks are all sub-blocks of the 3x3x3 region, offset by (0 or 1) in each dimension. Let me denote the 3x3x3 region as A[d][h][w] for d,h,w in 0..2: A[d][h][w] = input[n, c_in, 2*d_f+d, 2*h_f+h, 2*w_f+w] Then: S[7] = sum_{d=0..1, h=0..1, w=0..1} A[d][h][w] // (0,0,0) start S[6] = sum_{d=0..1, h=0..1, w=1..2} A[d][h][w] // (0,0,1) start S[5] = sum_{d=0..1, h=1..2, w=0..1} A[d][h][w] // (0,1,0) start S[4] = sum_{d=0..1, h=1..2, w=1..2} A[d][h][w] // (0,1,1) start S[3] = sum_{d=1..2, h=0..1, w=0..1} A[d][h][w] // (1,0,0) start S[2] = sum_{d=1..2, h=0..1, w=1..2} A[d][h][w] // (1,0,1) start S[1] = sum_{d=1..2, h=1..2, w=0..1} A[d][h][w] // (1,1,0) start S[0] = sum_{d=1..2, h=1..2, w=1..2} A[d][h][w] // (1,1,1) start We can compute these efficiently using prefix sums or just load each element once and add to the appropriate S[i]. For element A[d][h][w], it belongs to the blocks where start_d ≤ d < start_d+2, etc. Since start_d is 0 or 1, start_h is 0 or 1, start_w is 0 or 1: - A[0][0][0] belongs to blocks with start_d=0, start_h=0, start_w=0 → Just S[7] - A[0][0][1] belongs to blocks with start_d=0, start_h=0, start_w∈{0,1} → S[7], S[6] - A[0][0][2] belongs to blocks with start_d=0, start_h=0, start_w=1 → Just S[6] - A[1][1][1] belongs to all 8 blocks! Because start_d∈{0,1} (since 1 is in both [0,1] and [1,2]), similarly for h and w. Actually, element A[d][h][w] belongs to block (s_d, s_h, s_w) iff s_d ≤ d ≤ s_d+1, s_h ≤ h ≤ s_h+1, s_w ≤ w ≤ s_w+1. Since s_d ∈ {0,1}, and d ∈ {0,1,2}: - d=0: s_d must be 0 → contributes to blocks with s_d=0 (4 blocks) - d=1: s_d can be 0 or 1 → contributes to all 8 blocks - d=2: s_d must be 1 → contributes to blocks with s_d=1 (4 blocks) So A[1][1][1] contributes to all 8 blocks. A[0][0][0] contributes to 1 block. A[0][0][1] contributes to 2 blocks. A[0][1][1] contributes to 4 blocks. In any case, we can compute all 8 S[i] in 27 additions (each of the 27 elements added to 1, 2, 4, or 8 blocks). Actually, 27 elements, each added to 1-8 blocks. But wait, we can do it more cleverly. This is actually exactly the 3D prefix sum structure! And computing all 8 sums can be done efficiently.
